\=== POST 3 \===  
Rima Saha  
View Rima Saha’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Senior Software Engineer at Amazon | Expert in C/C++, Cloud Infrastructure, Networking & Security | Passionate about Scalable, High-Performance Systems | System Design

4d •   
4d Visible to everyone

Yesterday during a system design mock interview, something interesting happened.  
The candidate immediately started designing the system.  
📦 API Gateway  
📦 Load Balancer  
📦 Redis Cache  
📦 Kafka  
📦 Microservices  
Within minutes, the whiteboard looked impressive.

But then I asked a simple question.  
👉 “How many requests per second should this system handle?”  
There was a pause.  
We didn’t know:  
• How many users the system has  
• How often they use the product  
• How much data the system stores  
• What latency we are targeting  
And suddenly the architecture became guesswork.

This is one of the most common mistakes I see in system design interviews.

Candidates jump to architecture before understanding scale.  
Strong candidates usually start differently:  
1️⃣ Clarify requirements  
2️⃣ Estimate traffic and storage  
3️⃣ Identify bottlenecks  
4️⃣ Then design the architecture  
Something as simple as:  
10M users  
10% daily active  
10 actions per day  
→ \~115 requests/sec  
Now the design becomes grounded in reality.  
System design interviews are rarely about the perfect architecture.  
They are about how you think through the problem.

Curious to hear from others here 👇  
What is one mistake you keep seeing in system design interviews?  
\#systemdesign \#softwareengineering \#distributedsystems \#techinterviews \#backendengineering \#engineeringcareers

Fernando Franco  
View Fernando Franco’s profile  
   
• 1st  
PhD Senior Software Engineer | Algorithms, Distributed Systems, System Design, Computer Vision | Helping you to become a better software engineer

1w •   
1w Visible to everyone

There is a reason why Algorithms and Data Structures are hard for many software engineers.

It's not because they're not capable, but they simply miss a lot of pre-requisite knowledge.

If you want to learn dsa from first principles, read these 16 articles (links below):

1\. How depth-first search shines on directed graphs: topological sorting and strongly connected component algorithms  
https://lnkd.in/eSw2mnjh  
2\. Depth First Search and its applications on undirected graphs: finding cycles and articulation vertices.  
https://lnkd.in/eShkbN72  
3\. Breadth First Search and its applications: shortest paths, connected components and bipartite testing.  
https://lnkd.in/eZkHfhvN  
4\. A beginner friendly introduction to graphs. Different types of graphs and how to represent them.  
https://lnkd.in/eUwk5Y9v  
5\. What is a priority queue and how to implement it. Plus priority queues use cases and the heapsort algorithm.  
https://lnkd.in/eztWMfwN  
6\. Three must to know applications of the heap data structures: largest elements, graphs, and Huffmann encoding.  
https://lnkd.in/enfeNM-N  
7\. From traversal to reversal: essential linked list algorithms and techniques to solve common interview problems.  
https://lnkd.in/eYruabNt  
8\. How to quickly calculate range queries on arrays using prefix sum and sparse tables.  
https://lnkd.in/emgb2dA6  
9\. How to use the two-pointer method to efficiently solving array and string problems (with code templates).  
https://lnkd.in/eaxNapz4  
10\. How to effectively use sets and hash tables to solve coding interview problems.  
https://lnkd.in/e6j4gp2G  
11\. How to master backtracking algorithms: a step by step guide to solve any problem.  
https://lnkd.in/edwbW5uv

12\. Why comparison based algorithms cannot sort in less than O(nlogn). And why counting sort, radix sort and bucket sort can do better.  
https://lnkd.in/eTpk4rfB  
13\. How to efficiently store and represent strings using a trie data structure.  
https://lnkd.in/edNtV2yK  
14\. How to analyze the space complexity of an algorithm: time and space trade-offs and recursion.  
https://lnkd.in/eEB-e2P3  
15\. A 7 steps bulletproof framework to ace your next technical interview round.  
https://lnkd.in/ecEF-W3b  
16\. All you need to know about the array data structure, including multidimensional and dynamic arrays.  
https://lnkd.in/ekJMPDjy

2w •

JPMorganChase Compensation Range: SDE-3: ₹25-34 LPA | SDE-2: ₹18-24 LPA

Frequently Asked Technical Interview Questions:

𝗖𝗼𝗿𝗲 𝗝𝗮𝘃𝗮  
→ Approaches to sorting a Map in Java.  
→ Designing a thread-safe Singleton class.  
→ Key differences between Comparable and Comparator.  
→ Major enhancements introduced in Java 7 & 8; feature highlights of Java 8, 11, and 17\.  
→ Purpose and usage of try-with-resources.  
→ Multi-catch blocks and their advantages.  
→ Runnable vs Callable – execution and return handling.  
→ Categories of exceptions and Java’s exception hierarchy.  
→ Commonly implemented Design Patterns in Java applications.  
→ Core Object-Oriented Programming principles.  
→ Internal working of ConcurrentHashMap.  
→ Java Garbage Collection process and memory management.

𝗗𝗦𝗔/𝗖𝗼𝗱𝗶𝗻𝗴  
→ Detect duplicate strings within a collection.  
→ Determine whether a string is a palindrome.  
→ Combination Sum II using backtracking/recursion.  
→ Using Java Streams: filter odd numbers, apply a multiplier, and calculate aggregate sum.  
→ Identify the missing element in a consecutive integer array.  
→ Shift all zero elements to the end while maintaining order.  
→ Validate whether two strings are anagrams.  
→ Find the longest common prefix among multiple strings.  
→ Longest Increasing Subsequence (LIS).  
→ Maximum profit problem: Buy & Sell Stock.  
→ Implementation and complexity analysis of Dijkstra’s algorithm.  
→ Coin Change problem minimum coin computation.  
→ Reverse-and-add approach to palindrome generation.

𝗪𝗲𝗯/𝗙𝗿𝗮𝗺𝗲𝘄𝗼𝗿𝗸𝘀  
→ Architectural differences between REST and SOAP.  
→ Overview of the Spring ecosystem.  
→ Why enterprises adopt Spring.  
→ Spring vs Spring Boot configuration and deployment differences.  
→ Dependency injection and autowiring in Spring.  
→ Core concepts of Spring Security.  
→ Principles of RESTful API design.  
→ HTTP vs HTTPS \- protocol and security comparison.

𝗦𝘆𝘀𝘁𝗲𝗺 𝗗𝗲𝘀𝗶𝗴𝗻  
→ Present the architecture of a recent production-grade project.  
→ Designing a scalable fraud detection system.  
→ Database architecture for a ride-sharing platform.  
→ Data warehouse design for an e-commerce ecosystem.  
→ High-level design of a news aggregation platform.

𝗦𝗲𝗿𝘃𝗲𝗿/𝗦𝘆𝘀𝘁𝗲𝗺  
→ Diagnosing server crash root causes.  
→ Monitoring and analyzing server memory utilization.  
→ Investigating high CPU and memory consumption in JVM.  
→ Generating and interpreting heap dumps and thread dumps.

𝗞𝗲𝗲𝗽𝗶𝗻𝗴 𝘁𝗵𝗶𝘀 𝗶𝗻 𝗺𝗶𝗻𝗱, 𝗜’𝘃𝗲 𝗽𝗿𝗲𝗽𝗮𝗿𝗲𝗱 𝗶𝗻 𝗗𝗲𝗽𝘁𝗵 𝗝𝗮𝘃𝗮 𝗕𝗮𝗰𝗸𝗲𝗻𝗱 𝗗𝗲𝘃𝗲𝗹𝗼𝗽𝗲𝗿 𝗚𝘂𝗶𝗱𝗲.

𝗤𝘂𝗲𝘀𝘁𝗶𝗼𝗻: 𝗗𝗲𝘀𝗶𝗴𝗻 𝗮 𝗛𝗶𝘁 𝗖𝗼𝘂𝗻𝘁𝗲𝗿.

Follow-up: Optimizing the Trie for memory and adding weightage/frequency to suggestions.

System Logic: Strategy for zero-downtime key rotation in a Master-Slave architecture.

\=== POST 19 \===  
Nishant Singh  
View Nishant Singh’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Tech Lead | Distributed Systems | Cloud-Native Architecture | Observability | Java | SpringBoot | Event Driven |

1mo •   
1mo Visible to everyone

Java Multithreading Interview Questions Senior Engineers Still Get Asked in 2026

If you’re interviewing for Senior or Staff Java roles, multithreading interviews are rarely about definitions.  
Nobody really cares if you can recite what a thread is.

What interviewers actually look for:  
\- Have you handled concurrency in real systems?  
\- Do you understand trade-offs?  
\- Can you design thread-safe code without killing performance?

These Java multithreading questions keep coming up again and again.

Core Concepts (where many people stumble)  
\- When would you use Thread vs Runnable vs Callable in real code?  
\- What does volatile actually guarantee, and why it doesn’t make code thread-safe?  
\- How the Java Memory Model impacts visibility and ordering  
\- Why sleep() and wait() solve very different problems

Locks and Synchronization (this shows seniority)  
\- synchronized vs ReentrantLock and why both still matter  
\- Intrinsic locks vs explicit locks and when fairness is important  
\- How ReadWriteLock improves throughput and when it can backfire  
\- How deadlocks happen in production, not just in theory

Thread Pools and Executors (where most real bugs live)  
\- Choosing between fixed, cached, and work-stealing thread pools  
\- Sizing thread pools for CPU-bound vs IO-bound workloads  
\- What really happens when executor queues fill up  
\- When CompletableFuture improves clarity and when it hides complexity

Concurrent Collections (easy to misuse)  
\- Why ConcurrentHashMap scales better than synchronized collections  
\- What happens internally when multiple threads update the same map  
\- When CopyOnWriteArrayList is a good idea and when it’s a terrible one

Real-World Scenarios Interviewers Love  
\- Making a service thread-safe without destroying throughput  
\- Handling shared mutable state in high-traffic microservices  
\- Debugging race conditions that only appear in production

One honest interview tip:  
Strong candidates don’t just explain what works.  
They explain why they chose it, what could go wrong, and how they’d observe it in production.  
…see more

\=== POST 23 \===  
Addy Osmani  
View Addy Osmani’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Director, Google Cloud AI. Best-selling Author. Speaker. AI, DX, UX. I want to see you win.

1mo •   
1mo Visible to everyone

"Change what you're doing, to change where you're going"

Most of us know this intuitively, yet we live it daily. We want a different career trajectory but repeat the same work habits. We want to ship something meaningful but spend our evenings the same way we did last month. We want growth but cling to the familiar. 

The uncomfortable truth is that your current actions are a near-perfect predictor of your future outcomes. If you want a different destination, you have to change the route.

The tricky thing about loops is that they feel productive. You're busy. You're doing things. But activity isn't progress. Running on a treadmill burns calories, but it doesn't get you anywhere. The question isn't "Am I working hard?" \- it's "Am I working on the right things, in the right way, to get where I actually want to go?"

Identifying your unproductive loops requires honesty. Track how you actually spend your time for a week (not how you think you spend it). 

Look at the gap between your stated goals and your daily actions.   
That gap is your loop.

Knowing you need to change is easy. Actually changing is where most people stall \- because change means trading the comfort of the known for the discomfort of the unfamiliar. 

Here's what actually works:

1\. Get specific about the destination. Vague goals produce vague effort. "I want to grow my career" is a wish. "I want to lead a team of six within 18 months and ship a product used by 10,000 people" is a target you can reverse-engineer into weekly actions. The clearer the destination, the easier it is to see which current behaviors are taking you toward it and which are dead weight.

2\. Change one behavior at a time. Overhauling everything at once is a recipe for burning out by week three and snapping back to your old loop. Pick the single habit that would create the most leverage if changed. For a software engineer, that might be: stop waiting for perfect specs and start prototyping on day one. 

3\. Make the new behavior the path of least resistance. Willpower is finite. Instead of relying on discipline alone, restructure your environment. Want to write more? Open your draft before you open Slack each morning. Want to stop over-engineering? Set a hard rule: no design that takes longer to plan than to prototype. 

You don't need to have the whole journey mapped out. You just need to be honest about whether your current actions match your ambitions. If they don't, change the actions. The destination will follow.

The best behavior changes don't require you to be a better person \- they require a better system.

Illustration credit: GoLimitless & Shane Parrish

\#motivation \#productivity \#work \#growthmindsets  
…see more

\=== POST 29 \===  
Rohit Jain  
View Rohit Jain’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Ex-Amazon | IIT KGP 2019 alum | SDE @ Square | Insights on Interview Prep, System Design and Software Engineering

2mo •   
2mo Visible to everyone

Imagine you're sitting in an Interview for an SDE-II role at Amazon, and you're asked this question: "Design a distributed logging system."

I’m an Ex-Amazon backend engineer with over 7 years of experience...Here’s how I would break it down if I were the one being interviewed:

The real question here is not “which tool would you pick”. It’s whether you can design a high-throughput, highly reliable ingestion plus storage platform that stays alive during incidents, and still lets engineers debug fast.

There are lots of aspects we can cover here, but I’ll only cover the high-level design. 

1\. Requirements

The classic pain in distributed systems:  
\- Debugging is slow because logs are spread across machines  
\- Visibility is poor because you cannot see the whole system in one view  
\- Logging is inconsistent because formats and timestamps don’t match

Functional requirements  
\- Unified log collection from all services and environments  
\- Fast search and retrieval by service, time range, keywords  
\- Trace a single request end-to-end using a correlation ID  
\- Central dashboards for monitoring and alerting  
\- Retention and archival (hot vs cold) with policy-based purge

Non-functional requirements  
\- Low latency and minimally intrusive (logging must not slow down user requests)  
\- Horizontal scalability (add machines when volume grows)  
\- High availability and reliability (logs should not disappear during outages)  
\- Security and access control (logs can contain sensitive identifiers)

If you want numbers to anchor the discussion, I usually assume something like:  
\- Ingestion: hundreds of thousands of logs per second  
\- Hot storage growth: multiple TB per day  
\- Search load: hundreds of queries per second  
\- Availability target: five 9s is a great bar for “observability backbone”

2\. High-level architecture

I split the system into a clean pipeline:

\- Log producers (microservices)  
\- Log agents on every node (collection \+ masking \+ enrichment)  
\- Ingestion buffer (Kafka)  
\- Stream processing (Flink)  
\- Storage backends (search, metrics, archive)  
\- Query and dashboards (for humans and alerts)

3\. Correlation ID at the edge

First thing I want in the design: A correlation ID that is attached to the request at the entry point and flows through every service.

That single ID turns “random logs everywhere” into a timeline you can replay.

4\. Log agents on every machine

Think of them as the sidecar that does the boring but critical work:  
\- Tail logs locally  
\- Add metadata: service name, env, region, instance, version  
\- Mask obvious sensitive fields (still, never log secrets)  
\- Batch and ship asynchronously so the app never blocks

Common choices are Fluent Bit or Filebeat, but the tool name is not the point. The behavior is.

I’ve made a detailed video covering the whole design: https://lnkd.in/ejuw9tw2

You can read the rest of the post here: https://lnkd.in/eZSz8yWi

…see more

\=== POST 31 \===  
Aniraj Kumar  
View Aniraj Kumar’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
L5 @ Amazon || IIT Guwahati CSE

2mo •   
2mo Visible to everyone

Rubrik 𝗦𝗗𝗘-𝟮 𝗽𝗵𝗼𝗻𝗲𝘀𝗰𝗿𝗲𝗲𝗻 𝗶𝗻𝘁𝗲𝗿𝘃𝗶𝗲𝘄 𝗲𝘅𝗽𝗲𝗿𝗶𝗲𝗻𝗰𝗲

Walked into the interview thinking it’d be standard multithreading questions. Walked out realizing Rubrik really means systems-level thinking.  
𝗤𝘂𝗲𝘀𝘁𝗶𝗼𝗻 𝟭: Multithreaded Job Scheduler  
Tasks have dependencies (A → B → C).  
You’re given helper APIs like getDependentTasks() to discover the DAG. Starting task is returned when input is blank. Each task has a doWork() function to execute. Rules:  
\* Parent tasks must finish before child tasks start.  
\* If any task throws an exception → stop everything immediately  
\* No built-in Java concurrency libraries.  
\* Build your own blocking queue, thread pool, executor, using locks from scratch. Basically: “Here’s Java, now pretend java.util.concurrent never existed.” 

𝗤𝘂𝗲𝘀𝘁𝗶𝗼𝗻 𝟮: Parallel File Copy   
Given low-level APIs:  
pread(buffer, size, file)  
pwrite(buffer, size, file)  
Task: Copy data from one file to another in parallel using multiple threads. Had to design chunking, coordination, and thread safety manually.

𝗧𝗶𝗽𝘀: These interviews test real engineering depth, not just DSA patterns. Strong focus on concurrency, correctness, and failure handling 🙂   
I’ve set up a Topmate page to support anyone aiming for SDE roles. Click here to schedule a call\! https://lnkd.in/dwwvEkiK  
\#InterviewExperience \#Rubrik

Top JAVA and Spring Boot Interview Questions:

Java Core:  
\- How is Java platform independent (JVM vs JRE)?

\- Serializable interface

\- OOP vs scripting languages

\- Builder pattern (usage \+ example)  
\- Singleton implementation  
\- Factory pattern concept

\- Exception handling in controllers  
\- Transaction propagation  
\- application.properties purpose  
\- How Spring/Hibernate generates SQL  
\- MVC: Controller vs Service vs Repository  
\- Pagination and unique constraints

Collections:  
\- How HashMap works internally  
\- HashMap vs Hashtable vs ConcurrentHashMap  
\- List vs Set vs Map (when to use which)  
\- What is LinkedList  
\- Comparable interface  
\- How hashCode is generated  
\- Iterable interface

Streams API:  
\- What Streams API is  
\- Ways to create Streams  
\- Streams vs Collections  
\- Intermediate vs terminal operations  
\- map vs flatMap  
\- collect(), findFirst(), findAny()  
\- Exception handling in Streams  
\- Converting Stream to Collection  
\- Sequential vs parallel streams

Functional Programming:  
\- What is a functional interface  
\- Can it have default and static methods  
\- Common examples (Runnable, Comparator, Callable)

\=== POST 41 \===  
Puneet Patwari  
View Puneet Patwari’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Principal Software Engineer @Atlassian| Ex-Sr. Engineer @Microsoft || Sharing insights on SW Engineering, Career Growth & Interview Preparation

5mo •   
5mo Visible to everyone

I wish I had this checklist when I first started working as a backend engineer. Here are 7 mistakes that will slow you down if you ignore them.

1\. Overlooking Communication Protocols

↳ Knowing only how to make API calls isn’t enough. When you understand protocols like TCP, UDP, HTTP, and WebSockets, you start to see why certain requests are slow, how connections are made, and what causes timeouts.

↳ This foundation helps you make better decisions about performance, troubleshooting, and architecture.

↳ What to do: Read about how these protocols work under the hood, and notice how your services behave when you change connection settings or introduce new protocols.

2\. Treating Web Servers as a Setup Step

↳ Spinning up NGINX, Node.js, or Django is common, but it’s important to learn how these servers handle requests, manage connections, and serve content, static or dynamic.

↳ The way your server is configured has a direct impact on reliability, security, and speed, especially as your app grows.

↳ What to do: Experiment with server configs, study threading models, and look into how reverse proxies and load balancing fit in.

3\. Thinking Databases Are Only for Storage

↳ Databases do a lot more than just hold data. They provide consistency, support complex queries, manage concurrency, and help with data integrity.

↳ If you don’t understand concepts like indexing, normalization, or transaction handling, you’ll see slow queries, struggle with scaling, and run into bugs during migrations.

↳ What to do: Learn about ACID properties, indexing, and how SQL and NoSQL databases handle data differently. Dig into how data modeling can make or break performance.

4\. Not Paying Attention to Proxies and Load Balancers  
↳ Proxies aren’t just middlemen, they handle routing, security, and can help you scale by distributing traffic.

↳ If you skip learning about them, it’s easy to miss where traffic gets stuck or how to protect sensitive data.  
↳ What to do: Explore how proxies, reverse proxies, and load balancers work. Try setting them up and watch how they affect system behavior.

5\. Underestimating Caching

↳ Without caching, even a well-built backend can slow to a crawl as your user base grows.

↳ Understanding when and what to cache prevents unnecessary database load and reduces costs.

↳ What to do: Work with tools like Redis or Memcached, study cache invalidation strategies, and see the impact of caching on response times.

6\. Ignoring Messaging Systems

↳ Many systems need to coordinate tasks, send notifications, or process background jobs. Messaging systems like Kafka or RabbitMQ make this manageable.

↳ If you rely only on synchronous communication, your services become tightly coupled and harder to maintain.

↳ What to do: Set up a simple queue, send messages between services, and learn how pub/sub and queues help with scaling and reliability.

Continued in Comments…  
…see more

\=== POST 45 \===  
Raman Walia  
View Raman Walia’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Software Engineer at Meta | Follow for content on Software Engineering, Interview Prep and Dev Productivity

7mo •   
7mo Visible to everyone

I interviewed 17 SWEs who got recently hired, these 6 patterns were common in all of them:

(6th one is often discussed but less practiced)

\[1\] Use AI tools as an Interviewer

\- Ask the rating for your solution, get few tips to improve  
\- Write a prompt “Act as an Interviewer at Meta SWE Interview, ask 3 follow-up questions to my current solution for \[problem\]”

\[2\] Use AI tools as your coding buddy

\- Why my current solution is getting failed for this test case  
\- debug my current solution for this problem

\[3\] Build a spaced repetition log for hard problems

\- note down problems you faced problem in  
\- make a log for these problems like this:   
 “If I can’t solve it on day 1, I’ll revisit on day 3, then day 7”  
\- key is to remember the difficult patterns and retain them

\[4\] They record themselves while solving

\- I have tried this myself  
\- Explain problems to the camera like you are doing it to Interviewer  
\- Have a 30-45 mins timer for this

\[5\] Build mini side projects using DSA concepts

\- Build a Trie autocomplete tool  
\- Implement a heap-powered task scheduler

This makes your DSA learnings memorable and portfolio-ready

\[6\] Do a lot of mock interviews 

\- Use online (free) platforms for mocks   
\- Do as many as you can, but at least 10 before the actual interview

\=== POST 52 \===  
Rajya Vardhan Mishra  
View Rajya Vardhan Mishra’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Engineering Leader @ Google | Mentored 300+ Software Engineers | Building high-performance teams | Tech Speaker | Led $1B+ programs | Cornell University | Lifelong learner driven by optimism & growth mindset

10mo •   
10mo Visible to everyone

If you want to become a better backend engineer, spend you time on these 10 fundamentals. 

After spending 19+ years across Google, Amazon & various startups, these are the topics in my opinion that one should focus on:

1/ Communication Protocols   
 \- TCP (reliable, connection-oriented) vs. UDP (faster, connectionless).   
 \+ Differences in use cases, performance, and reliability.   
 \- Evolution of HTTP (HTTP/1.1 → HTTP/2 → HTTP/3).   
 \+ Multiplexing, streams, and the role of QUIC in HTTP/3.   
 \- Real-time communication protocols: WebSockets, gRPC, raw TCP/UDP.   
 \+ When to use them (e.g., chat apps, gaming). 

2/ Web Servers   
 \- Role of web servers like NGINX, Apache, or custom-built ones.   
 \+ How they process requests and manage threads/connections.   
 \- HTTP protocol configurations (HTTP/1.1, HTTP/2, HTTP/3).   
 \+ Performance considerations like multiplexing and persistent connections.   
 \- Proxy setups (forward/reverse proxies).   
 \+ CDN as a reverse proxy and API gateways for backend management. 

3/ Database Engineering   
 \- ACID properties (Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability).   
 \+ Why they matter and trade-offs for performance.   
 \- Relational vs. Non-relational databases.   
 \+ Examples: PostgreSQL, MongoDB, Cassandra, Redis for Caching.   
 \- Indexing techniques.   
 \+ B+ Trees, LSM, and query optimization. 

4/ Proxies   
\- Explore how proxies improve scalability and security.   
 \- Elements to be covered:   
 \- Forward proxy vs. Reverse proxy.   
 \+ Use cases in caching, authentication, and load balancing.   
 \- Layer 4 (transport layer) vs. Layer 7 (application layer) proxies.   
 \+ How they interact with protocols like HTTP and TCP.   
 \- Examples of popular proxies: NGINX, HAProxy, Envoy. 

5/ Messaging Systems   
 \- Publish-Subscribe model.   
 \+ Examples: Kafka (long-polling), RabbitMQ (push).   
 \- Message delivery guarantees.   
 \+ At-least-once, exactly-once, at-most-once delivery.   
 \- Real-world applications in microservices and event-driven architecture. 

6/ Message Formats   
 \- Human-readable formats: JSON, XML.   
 \+ When to use and their pros/cons.   
 \- Binary formats: Protocol Buffers, Avro.   
 \+ Faster serialization/deserialization for large-scale systems. 

7/ Security   
 \- Encryption and secure communication (TLS/SSL).   
 \+ Preventing man-in-the-middle attacks.   
 \- Common vulnerabilities:   
 \+ XSS, SQL injection, and how to mitigate them.   
 \- DDOS prevention techniques.   
 \+ Use of firewalls and layer 7 DDOS protection tools like Cloudflare. 

8/ Load Balancing and Scaling   
 \- Load balancing techniques.   
 \+ Round-robin, weighted load balancing, least connections.   
 \- Horizontal vs. vertical scaling.   
 \+ Pros/cons and real-world scenarios.   
 \- Autoscaling in cloud environments.   
 \+ AWS, GCP, and Azure implementations. 

Continued in comments ↓

\--  
P.S: What are you starting with first on this list? Don't forget to repost and share this post\!

Most down-leveling decisions happen because of behavioral interview performance, not technical gaps. While you might ace the system design, a weak behavioral showing can cost you the level, or even the offer entirely.

Companies want to understand not only that you have the technical skills, but that you have experience and effectiveness that will translate to their culture and environment.

To nail this interview, it helps to understand how behavioral interviews really work behind the scenes. Dig into this often-misunderstood interview type and what it means for you as you prepare in our latest substack\!

\=== POST 55 \===  
Srishtik Dutta  
View Srishtik Dutta’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
SWE-2 @Google | Ex \- Microsoft, Wells Fargo | ACM ICPC ’20 Regionalist | 6🌟 at Codechef | Expert at Codeforces | Guardian (Top 1%) on LeetCode | Technical Content Writer ✍️| 125K+ on LinkedIn

10mo •   
10mo Visible to everyone

Maths in CP Unlocked \- Topics and Resources to master the maths in DSA/CP...

Maths is always the fundamental of Computer Science and one of the core reasons why some folks learn CP faster than the others. Having a strong background in Maths helped me get better at CP / DSA faster and crack my roles at Wells Fargo, Google and Microsoft.

While maths is infinite and can't be contained in a syllabus, for the interview level at FAANG and the coding contests like ICPC \- International Collegiate Programming Contest, I've compiled the resources and topics one needs to cover for mastering the core mathematics behind DSA problems : 

1\. Basic Maths : Having a good grasp of all the 10+2 concepts like algebra, equation solving, coordinate geometry, maxima \- minima, vectors and 3d geometry would be a great starting point for maths in any CS based roles.   
Resources:   
NCERT Books for 11- 12 : https://lnkd.in/gawd\_jMv

2\. Number Theory : Number theory is a crucial topics in interviews and coding contests and topics can range from GCD, Prime factorization algorithms, Sieve to advanced algos like Mobius Functions and Chinese Remainder Theorum.  
Resources:  
1\. CP Algorithms Site for basics : https://lnkd.in/guFBA5Qv  
2\. Vivek Gupta Diophathine Equations : https://lnkd.in/gP--wSDE  
3\. Colin Galen Number theory topic streams: https://lnkd.in/gpNcGzvN  
4\. Utkarsh Gupta crash course for maths for DSA / CP : https://lnkd.in/gpSyPexX

3\. Combinatorics, Probability and Expected Value : One of the most important topics for the harder interviews like Google and HFTs. Involves topics from simple nCr, Pascals Triangle, Linearity of expectations, to advanced stuffs like Lucas's theorum, DP with combinatorics, and Markov's chains.  
Resources :   
1\. Vivek Gupta CSES problem set solved : https://lnkd.in/gxXXWtHH  
2\. Colin Galen topic streams: https://lnkd.in/gPBRUdEW  
3\. Cp algorithms blogs : https://lnkd.in/g5Y3wHzt

Bonus :  
4\. Advanced Maths : Advanced maths includes advanced topics like Generating functions, Polynomial Interpolation, Berlekamp Massey, Discrete Derivates, FFTs, and more. This is the kind of problems for the ICPC Asia West and World Finals and if you're already at this level, you don't need this post. The resources are mostly CodeForces blogs.

Maths is mostly a matter of practice and efforts. Once it's mastered, solving a lot of CP problems gets easier and the journey clears up. Keep practicing a lot of problems to see the results.

Stay tuned for more such content\!\!✌🏻🚀

Personal DSA Sheet: https://lnkd.in/gnH8d4Dh  
…see more

𝐋𝐚𝐧𝐝𝐞𝐝 𝐚𝐧 𝐒𝐃𝐄-𝟑 𝐫𝐨𝐥𝐞 𝐚𝐭 𝐀𝐝𝐨𝐛𝐞 – 𝐇𝐞𝐫𝐞’𝐬 𝐡𝐨𝐰 𝐢𝐭 𝐰𝐞𝐧𝐭\!

The entire interview journey was thoughtful, challenging, and incredibly rewarding. If you're preparing for SDE roles—especially in product-based companies—I hope this breakdown helps you out:

𝐑𝐨𝐮𝐧𝐝 𝟏: 𝐎𝐧𝐥𝐢𝐧𝐞 𝐀𝐬𝐬𝐞𝐬𝐬𝐦𝐞𝐧𝐭 (𝐇𝐚𝐜𝐤𝐞𝐫𝐑𝐚𝐧𝐤)  
Two engaging problems:  
➤ Task Scheduler with Cooldown: Optimize task execution while respecting cooldown intervals between similar tasks  
➤ Bounded Buffer Simulation: Simulate a producer-consumer system with buffer limits and concurrent handling

𝐑𝐨𝐮𝐧𝐝 𝟐: 𝐃𝐚𝐭𝐚 𝐒𝐭𝐫𝐮𝐜𝐭𝐮𝐫𝐞𝐬 & 𝐀𝐥𝐠𝐨𝐫𝐢𝐭𝐡𝐦𝐬 (𝐋𝐢𝐯𝐞 𝐂𝐨𝐝𝐢𝐧𝐠)  
➤ Problem: Find the maximum number of overlapping intervals (using sweep line algorithm)  
➤ Follow-up: Optimized complexity and explained edge-case handling

𝐑𝐨𝐮𝐧𝐝 𝟑: 𝐋𝐨𝐰-𝐋𝐞𝐯𝐞𝐥 𝐃𝐞𝐬𝐢𝐠𝐧 (𝐋𝐋𝐃)  
➤ Designed a Token Bucket-based Rate Limiter  
➤ Discussed object-oriented modeling, concurrency control, Redis-based scaling, and unit testing strategy

𝐑𝐨𝐮𝐧𝐝 𝟒: 𝐇𝐢𝐫𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐌𝐚𝐧𝐚𝐠𝐞𝐫 𝐑𝐨𝐮𝐧𝐝  
➤ Walked through a critical refactor of a real-time messaging system  
➤ Covered: Navigating ambiguity, meeting deadlines, and technical communication with stakeholders

𝐑𝐨𝐮𝐧𝐝 𝟓: 𝐁𝐚𝐫 𝐑𝐚𝐢𝐬𝐞𝐫 / 𝐕𝐏 𝐃𝐢𝐬𝐜𝐮𝐬𝐬𝐢𝐨𝐧  
➤ Discussed prioritization of long-term code health vs. short-term deliveries  
➤ Touched on team leadership, mentorship, and aligning engineering goals with business outcomes

𝐓𝐚𝐤𝐞𝐚𝐰𝐚𝐲𝐬:

System design is about understanding real-world trade-offs, not just drawing boxes  
Clarity in communication often matters more than just arriving at the correct solution  
Cultural alignment and ownership mindset are critical at senior levels  
If you're preparing for mid-level or senior SDE interviews, feel free to reach out. Happy to connect, mock interview, or share my prep strategy\!

I've been mentored by many top software engineers in the industry.

They don't even know who I am. 

\- I learned scaling distributed systems from Jeff Dean by reading his papers on Bigtable and Spanner  
\- I learned how to build modern bundlers from Evan You by reading Vite's source code  
\- I learned how React works from Dan Abramov and Sebastian Markbage by reading React's source code  
\- I learned JavaScript from Addy Osmani by reading patterns.dev and his book on JavaScript Design Patterns  
\- I learned how to build testing frameworks like Jest from Christoph Nakazawa by reading his blog cpojer.net  
\- I learned software development from Martin Fowler's by reading martinfowler.com

Many influential people have shared their experiences, expertise, and knowledge online in the form of papers, code, articles, talks. Most are free for anyone to access.

Direct mentorship is valuable but hard to come by. Indirect mentorship is readily available if you bother to look.

With GreatFrontEnd.com, I’m turning years of knowledge into structured content so I can mentor thousands of front-end engineers – even if we never meet.

📌 𝗜’𝘃𝗲 𝗿𝗲𝗰𝗲𝗶𝘃𝗲𝗱 𝗼𝗳𝗳𝗲𝗿𝘀 𝗳𝗿𝗼𝗺 𝗠𝗲𝘁𝗮, 𝗨𝗯𝗲𝗿, 𝗖𝗼𝗶𝗻𝗯𝗮𝘀𝗲, 𝗥𝗶𝗽𝗽𝗹𝗶𝗻𝗴, 𝗟𝗶𝗻𝗸𝗲𝗱𝗜𝗻, 𝗠𝗶𝗰𝗿𝗼𝘀𝗼𝗳𝘁, 𝗙𝗹𝗶𝗽𝗸𝗮𝗿𝘁, 𝗚𝗼𝗼𝗴𝗹𝗲, 𝗦𝗮𝗹𝗲𝘀𝗳𝗼𝗿𝗰𝗲, 𝗮𝗻𝗱 𝘀𝘁𝗮𝗿𝘁𝘂𝗽𝘀 𝗜 𝗰𝗮𝗻’𝘁 𝗲𝘃𝗲𝗻 𝗿𝗲𝗺𝗲𝗺𝗯𝗲𝗿.

And this is: H𝗼𝘄 𝘁𝗼 𝘄𝗿𝗶𝘁𝗲 𝗮 𝗿𝗲𝘀𝘂𝗺𝗲 𝘁𝗵𝗮𝘁 𝗮𝗰𝘁𝘂𝗮𝗹𝗹𝘆 𝗴𝗲𝘁𝘀 𝘆𝗼𝘂 𝗼𝗳𝗳𝗲𝗿𝘀, 𝗡𝗢𝗧 𝗷𝘂𝘀𝘁 𝗶𝗻𝘁𝗲𝗿𝘃𝗶𝗲𝘄𝘀.

I’m not here to tell you what you're doing wrong.  
I’m here to show you how to do it right.

I got those interviews and offers because of my resume, NOT in spite of it.

Everything I’m sharing here comes from personal experience.  
These are not recycled tips from the internet or borrowed frameworks from career coaches.  
𝗧𝗵𝗶𝘀 𝗶𝘀 𝘄𝗵𝗮𝘁 𝗜’𝘃𝗲 𝘁𝗿𝗶𝗲𝗱, 𝘁𝗲𝘀𝘁𝗲𝗱, 𝗿𝗲𝗳𝗶𝗻𝗲𝗱, 𝗮𝗻𝗱 𝗶𝘁 𝘄𝗼𝗿𝗸𝘀.  
𝗦𝗼𝗺𝗲 𝗼𝗳 𝗶𝘁 𝗺𝗮𝘆 𝘀𝗼𝘂𝗻𝗱 𝗿𝗮𝗱𝗶𝗰𝗮𝗹. 𝗧𝗵𝗮𝘁’𝘀 𝗯𝗲𝗰𝗮𝘂𝘀𝗲 𝗶𝘁 𝗶𝘀.  
R𝗮𝗱𝗶𝗰𝗮𝗹 𝗰𝗹𝗮𝗿𝗶𝘁𝘆 𝗶𝘀 𝗯𝗲𝘁𝘁𝗲𝗿 𝘁𝗵𝗮𝗻 𝗹𝘂𝗸𝗲𝘄𝗮𝗿𝗺 𝗳𝗹𝘂𝗳𝗳.

Every resume should be optimized in two distinct ways:

🔹 𝟭. 𝗢𝗽𝘁𝗶𝗺𝗶𝘇𝗲𝗱 𝗳𝗼𝗿 𝗴𝗲𝘁𝘁𝗶𝗻𝗴 𝗶𝗻𝘁𝗲𝗿𝘃𝗶𝗲𝘄𝘀  
– Use LaTeX (you’re not in school anymore; stop submitting Word docs).  
– Customize for every role.  
– Quality \>\>\> Quantity.  
– You’re not swiping right on jobs. You’re curating your presence.  
– A generic resume \= a wasted opportunity.

🔹 𝟮. 𝗢𝗽𝘁𝗶𝗺𝗶𝘇𝗲𝗱 𝗳𝗼𝗿 𝘁𝗵𝗲 𝗵𝗶𝗿𝗶𝗻𝗴 𝗰𝗼𝗺𝗺𝗶𝘁𝘁𝗲𝗲  
– Role → Task → Result (RTR)  
– Think like a hiring manager. What proof would you want to see?  
– Your resume should de-risk you as a false positive.  
– Don’t write fluff. If you don’t have impact stories, go do things that create them.

💡 𝗦𝗼𝗺𝗲 𝗵𝗮𝗿𝗱 𝘁𝗿𝘂𝘁𝗵𝘀:

𝗡𝗼𝗻-𝗡𝗲𝗴𝗼𝘁𝗶𝗮𝗯𝗹𝗲𝘀:  
✔ Good experience  
✔ Good projects  
✔ Proactivity  
✔ Performance beyond your current level

𝗡𝗲𝗴𝗼𝘁𝗶𝗮𝗯𝗹𝗲𝘀:  
✖️ Your GPA  
✖️ Your University  
✖️ Your past company

𝗟𝗲𝘁 𝗺𝗲 𝗯𝗲 𝘃𝗲𝗿𝘆 𝗰𝗹𝗲𝗮𝗿:  
This is 𝗡𝗢𝗧 about “beating the ATS.”  
This is about being undeniable when a human being reads your resume.

No softness. No vague encouragement.  
Just clarity, edge, and outcomes.

🎥 𝗜’𝗹𝗹 𝗯𝗲 𝗿𝗲𝗹𝗲𝗮𝘀𝗶𝗻𝗴 𝗮 𝗳𝘂𝗹𝗹 𝘃𝗶𝗱𝗲𝗼 𝗯𝗿𝗲𝗮𝗸𝗱𝗼𝘄𝗻 𝗼𝗻 𝗺𝘆 𝗬𝗼𝘂𝗧𝘂𝗯𝗲 𝘀𝗼𝗼𝗻.  
𝗜𝘁’𝗹𝗹 𝘄𝗮𝗹𝗸 𝘆𝗼𝘂 𝘁𝗵𝗿𝗼𝘂𝗴𝗵:

– What to write  
– How to frame your impact  
– What gets flagged at hiring committee  
– Why some people never get callbacks (and how to not be them)

👇 𝗨𝗻𝘁𝗶𝗹 𝘁𝗵𝗲𝗻, 𝗱𝗿𝗼𝗽 𝗾𝘂𝗲𝘀𝘁𝗶𝗼𝗻𝘀 𝗶𝗻 𝘁𝗵𝗲 𝗰𝗼𝗺𝗺𝗲𝗻𝘁𝘀.  
If there’s a term or point I mentioned that you didn’t quite get, ask. Always better to ask a friend than to stay confused in front of the world and here, I’m that friend.

See you on YouTube.  
𝗔𝗻𝗱 𝗿𝗲𝗺𝗲𝗺𝗯𝗲𝗿: 𝗗𝗼𝗻’𝘁 𝘄𝗿𝗶𝘁𝗲 𝘁𝗼 𝗳𝗶𝗹𝗹 𝘀𝗽𝗮𝗰𝗲. 𝗪𝗿𝗶𝘁𝗲 𝘁𝗼 𝗲𝗮𝗿𝗻 𝘆𝗼𝘂𝗿 𝗽𝗹𝗮𝗰𝗲.

\=== POST 62 \===  
Prafful Agarwal  
View Prafful Agarwal’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Software Engineer at Google

11mo •   
11mo Visible to everyone

Give me 2 minutes. I’ll give you back the 90 minutes of notes I made on how OAuth 2.0 works.

♦ What is OAuth 2.0?

OAuth 2.0 is an open standard for authorization used to provide secure, delegated access to resources without sharing login credentials.

It allows third-party applications to request limited access to user accounts without exposing the user's password. 

OAuth 2.0 is widely adopted by platforms like Google, Facebook, GitHub, and many others to grant third-party apps controlled access to their users' data.

♦ Where is OAuth 2.0 Used?

OAuth 2.0 is commonly used in situations like:  
\- Allowing third-party apps to access social media profiles without needing to log in directly.  
\- Enabling integrations between software services, such as allowing an app to post content on your behalf on a social platform.  
\- Granting access to APIs, such as those used by cloud services or financial platforms.

♦ How Does OAuth 2.0 Work?

OAuth 2.0 involves interactions between four primary components: the Client, Authorization Server, Resource Server, and the Resource Owner (the user). 

Here’s a simplified step-by-step of how it works:

 1\. Authorization Grant Request  
 \- Client (the app) requests permission from the Resource Owner (user) to access protected resources. This can happen when a user tries to log in or link an account.   
 \- The Authorization Server provides an Authorization Grant to the Client (Step A in the diagram).

 2\. Access Token & Refresh Token  
 \- The Authorization Grant is validated, and the Client receives an Access Token and, sometimes, a Refresh Token (Step B).   
 \- The Access Token is used to access protected resources on behalf of the user, while the Refresh Token can be used to request a new access token when the old one expires.

 3\. Accessing Resources  
 \- The Client sends the Access Token to the Resource Server to request access to specific resources (Step C).  
 \- The Resource Server verifies the token and, if valid, allows access (Step D).

 4\. Token Validation  
 \- If the token is invalid or expired, the Resource Server responds with an Invalid Token Error (Step F).  
 \- The client must then either obtain a new token or refresh the existing one.

 5\. Token Refresh  
 \- If the Access Token expires, the Client can use the Refresh Token to request a new Access Token from the Authorization Server (Step G).  
 \- This process does not require the user to log in again and extends the session.

 6\. Renewal and Optional Refresh  
 \- After the refresh, a new Access Token (and sometimes a Refresh Token) is provided for further use (Step H).

TL;DR: 

1\. Authorization Grant: Client requests permission from the user.  
2\. Access Token Issued: Authorization server issues access and refresh tokens.  
3\. Protected Resources Accessed: Client uses the token to access resources.  
4\. Token Refresh: Tokens are refreshed automatically when expired.  
…see more

@Hopsworks || Database Internals | DB Kernel Enthusiast

11mo •   
11mo Visible to everyone

🔍How Many B+Tree Searches Are Hidden Inside a MySQL Insert❓  
1️⃣ What is a B+Tree Search?  
 \* A B+Tree search starts from the root page and traverses down level by level, comparing keys until the target page at the specified level is located. Typically, the target level is 0 (leaf level), but during page splits or merges, the parent level may also be involved.  
 \* B+Tree search is a core operation in InnoDB's B+Tree handling. The key function is 'btr\_cur\_search\_to\_nth\_level()'. Insert, update, delete, and read operations all require B+Tree searches.  
 \* During a search, latches are acquired depending on the operation type. A read typically only latches the leaf page (S latch). For writes, optimistic inserts latch the leaf page (X latch), while pessimistic inserts may X latch the entire subtree involved in a page split or merge.  
 \* The cost of a B+Tree search includes potential page reads from disk, page latching and index locking, and key comparisons at each level.

2️⃣ Number of B+Tree Searches When Inserting into a Primary Key Index  
 \* 1st search: Locate the leaf page where the new record should be inserted. If there's enough space, insert directly. Otherwise:  
 \* 2nd search: Latch the subtree involved and split the leaf page, then insert.  
 \* 3rd search: Locate the parent page of the split leaf and insert a pointer to the new page. If the parent page has enough space, insert directly. Otherwise, split the parent page:  
 \* Extra searches: If the parent page also needs to split, recursively search and update higher-level parent pages, up to the root. This could require up to (B+Tree height \- 2\) additional searches.  
 \* 4th search: If the record contains large BLOB fields stored externally, locate the newly inserted record again to insert the external BLOB, and update the external reference.

➔ Maximum number of B+Tree searches: 5 \+ (B+Tree height \- 2\)

3️⃣ Number of B+Tree Searches When Inserting into a Unique Secondary Index  
 \* 1st search: Locate the leaf page to find the appropriate insertion position and check for potential duplicate keys. If potential duplicates are found:   
 \* 2nd search: Locate the first candidate and perform a rightward lock scan (Next-Key Locking) to check for delete-marked records. If a non-deleted duplicate is found, return a duplicate key error. Otherwise, continue scanning to the right until encountering the first non-matching record, at which point the scan stops.  
 \* 3rd search: Re-locate the correct position to insert after ensuring no duplicates. If the leaf page lacks space:  
 \* 4th search: Latch the subtree and split the page.  
 \* 5th search: Locate the parent page to insert a new pointer for the split page. If the parent page is full, recursively split:  
 \* Extra searches: As with the primary key, this may cascade upward, requiring up to (B+Tree height \- 2\) additional searches.

➔ Maximum number of B+Tree searches: 6 \+ (B+Tree height \- 2\)

…see more

This is the resume I used, to apply at Google. It has always been a one page resume. 

These are some points which you should keep in mind while creating your resume:

✅ What to Do in a Tech Resume

\- Add a Job Title Below Your Name: This sets context instantly (e.g., “Full Stack Developer – Node.js & React”).

\- Prioritize Recent, Relevant Roles: Focus on the last few years of experience that align with the role you're applying for.

\- Stick to a Clean Structure: Role → Company → Dates → Key points → Result. Avoid clutter.

\- Tailor Your Tech Stack: Only list tools you're confident in and that match the job description.

\- Include Personal or Open-Source Projects: These show initiative and real-world application of your skills.

\- Use Metrics to Show Impact: Think "Improved deployment speed by 50%" vs. "Worked on deployments."

\- Explain Collaboration Briefly: Mention how you worked with designers, PMs, or engineers to solve problems.

❌ What to Avoid in a Tech Resume

\- Don’t Use Internal Jargon: Avoid company-specific terms or acronyms that won’t make sense outside.

\- Don’t Use Paragraphs for Work Descriptions: Use bullets—no one reads long blocks of text.

\- Don’t List Tools You Barely Know: If you can’t answer follow-up questions on it, don’t include it.

\- Don’t Add Unnecessary Personal Details: Your resume doesn’t need your full address, age, or marital status.

\- Don’t Mention Every Job You’ve Ever Had: Leave out unrelated or outdated roles that don’t add value.

\- Don’t Overuse Buzzwords: Avoid vague claims like “hard-working” or “results-driven” without proof.

\- Don’t Overpromise: Be honest about your level of experience—it's okay to say “learning” or “basic knowledge.”

\- Don’t Submit Without Testing the Format: Save and view your resume as a PDF on different devices to make sure it reads cleanly everywhere.

\=== POST 69 \===  
sukhad anand  
View sukhad anand’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Senior Software Engineer @Google | Techie007 | Opinions and views I post are my own

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

Apache Kafka summarized in 10 points:

1\. Apache Kafka is a Distributed, Replicated Messaging Queue. It functions like a commit log.

2\. It is completely open source and you can download it directly. But to use it, you need to create an Apache Kafka cluster. Because running Apache Kafka on just one system won’t work in a distributed environment.

3\. A cluster of Apache Kafka contains multiple servers. Each server is called a broker and stores the data. But where is the data stored? Apache Kafka makes use of secondary storage for storing the data. A lot of people have apprehensions about hard disks being slower than the main memory. You can make this access faster by writing and reading from sequential memory locations rather than random locations. This is how Kafka stores data. How is this access sequential ? We will see below.

4\. Regarding storage in Kafka, you’ll always hear two terms — Partition and Topic. Partitions are the units of storage in Kafka for messages. Topic can be thought of as being a container in which these partitions lie.

5\. Whenever you create a topic in Kafka, it creates the directories equal to the number of partitions you have specified — One directory for one partition of the topic. In Kafka, the topic is more of a logical grouping than anything else, and that the Partition is the actual unit of storage in Kafka. That is what is physically stored on the disk.

6\. Each partition is further subdivided into segments. Each segment is a log file containing the incoming messages. Each message which is stored in the log file contains the actual message along with the offset(number of messages in the file \+ 1\) at which it occurs.

7\. Kafka writes to any of the topic partitions ,if we want to maintain some ordering we can add the hashing key to write in the same partition . Each partition can be consumed by only one consumers with in a same consumers group but a different consumer can read from same partitions .(this is a Kafka requirement).

8\. A common operation in Kafka is to read the message at a particular offset. How will you find this offset? Scanning the log file? But, it will take a lot of time. This is where the index file comes to help which stores the physical address for each offset.

9\. Kafka does not always access disk sequentially but it does some things that make it much more likely that disk access is often sequential. All Kafka messages are stored in larger segment files and since Kafka messages are not deleted when consumed (like in other message brokers) Kafka will not end up creating a fragmented filesystem over time by continuously creating and deleting many variable length files.

10\. Instead it creates segment files and then appends them to that file until it reaches 1GB(configurable). When all messages in the segment expire, it deletes the entire segment.  
\_\_

I recently created a detailed video explaining Kafka and also why is it fast ?

Link in the first comment.

\=== POST 71 \===  
Rajya Vardhan Mishra  
View Rajya Vardhan Mishra’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Engineering Leader @ Google | Mentored 300+ Software Engineers | Building high-performance teams | Tech Speaker | Led $1B+ programs | Cornell University | Lifelong learner driven by optimism & growth mindset

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

In the last 15 years, I’ve interviewed at Google, Microsoft, Paytm, Amazon, and dozens of startups. And no matter the company, the interview pattern rarely changes. 

Here’s what to expect in 99% of product-based company interviews: 

 1\. Data Structures & Algorithms (DSA)   
Time: 45-60 minutes 

What happens:   
\- Solve 1-2 DSA problems within the time limit.   
\- Write optimal, bug-free code that actually runs.   
\- Explain time & space complexity for every approach.   
\- Be prepared to handle edge cases, one miss can cost you. 

DSA is usually the first filter. If you fail here, you don’t move forward. 

 2\. System Design / High-Level Design (HLD)   
Time: 60-90 minutes 

What happens:   
\- You get an open-ended system design problem.   
\- Explain the architecture, database modeling, and API contracts.   
\- Think about scalability, performance, and fault tolerance.   
\- No coding, just whiteboarding & deep technical discussions. 

There’s no single correct answer, what matters is how you think and justify your design. 

 3\. Low-Level Design (LLD)   
Time: 60-90 minutes 

What happens:   
\- You get a generic feature to design   
\- (like a parking lot system, ATM, or rate limiter).   
\- Define classes, attributes, and interactions.   
\- Follow SOLID principles and avoid redundant data.   
\- Sometimes, you’ll need to write actual code based on the discussion. 

Good design choices can make or break your interview. 

 4\. Machine Coding Round (MC)   
Time: 90-180 minutes 

What happens:   
\- Build a working solution for a given problem, this isn’t about theory anymore.   
\- Write executable, maintainable code for at least one core functionality.   
\- Follow best practices, clean code, SOLID, design patterns, error handling.   
\- Your code should be flexible enough for modifications (they might ask you to extend functionality). 

5\. Managerial Discussion   
Time: 45-60 minutes 

What happens:   
\- Deep dive into your past projects, expect follow-up questions.   
\- How did you solve challenges? What was your impact?   
\- Situational questions (answered best with the STAR method).   
\- You’re evaluated on ownership, leadership, and decision-making. 

This round is not just about tech, it’s about how you work and think. 

 6\. Cultural Fit Round   
Time: 30-45 minutes 

What happens:   
\- How well do you align with the company’s values & culture?   
\- Expect behavioral questions (e.g., “Tell me about a time you handled conflict”).   
\- Be honest, don’t make up stories to fit the values. 

You can clear every technical round and still get rejected here.   
   
Also, Some companies include:   
\- Concurrency discussions (how to handle multi-threading & parallelism).   
\- Pair programming (coding in real-time with an interviewer).   
\- Take-home assignments (especially in startups). 

But for most product-based companies, these six rounds are the standard.   
…see more

Ruby M

PayPal Interview Experience – Technical Round (3-4 Years Experience, 45 mins)

Core Java & Data Structures \-  
Explain the internal working of ConcurrentHashMap. How does it achieve thread safety, and what are its performance trade-offs?  
Describe the differences between WeakHashMap and HashMap. When would you use each?  
Given a large dataset (millions of records), how would you efficiently search for duplicate transactions?  
How does the Java Memory Model impact multi-threaded applications?  
Design an in-memory key-value store that supports TTL (time to live) for entries.

Multithreading & Concurrency   
How would you design a thread pool from scratch?  
Explain how Java handles false sharing in multi-core processors.  
What are the main drawbacks of using synchronized methods? How does ReentrantLock improve performance?  
Implement a multi-threaded rate limiter that handles API requests.  
How would you handle thread starvation in high-throughput applications?

Spring Boot & Microservices   
How does Spring Boot’s auto-configuration determine which beans to load?  
What is Circuit Breaker, and how does it prevent cascading failures in microservices?  
How would you handle blue-green deployments in a Spring Boot microservices setup?  
Explain Eventual Consistency. How would you ensure data consistency across distributed services?  
Implement a rate-limited API in Spring Boot that handles 10,000 requests per second.

APIs & RESTful Services   
How would you design an API Gateway to handle dynamic routing and security policies?  
Explain the challenges of handling pagination in REST APIs for massive datasets.  
How would you manage API timeouts and retries in a distributed system?  
What’s the best way to implement WebSockets in a fintech application?  
How would you enforce idempotency in payment APIs?

System Design Basics   
Design a high-throughput, low-latency order-matching system for a stock exchange.  
How would you ensure data integrity in a multi-region database setup?  
Explain Leader Election. How would you implement it in a microservices-based system?  
What are the trade-offs between CQRS and traditional CRUD-based systems?  
How does a Distributed Message Queue (like Kafka) handle backpressure?

tip: They look for scalability thinking, real world problem solving, and strong system design intuition. If you’re aiming for backend-heavy fintech roles, focus on concurrency, API optimizations, and microservices resilience patterns. And don’t forget system design

Software Engineering cheatsheet I always wanted to have for interviews (this is a game changer\!)

1yr Visible to everyone

If you are interviewing for a junior Java engineer job role, you must know the answer to the following questions.

\- What is the difference between JDK, JRE, and JVM?  
\- What is the difference between \== and equals() in Java?  
\- Explain the concept of Java Object Oriented Programming (OOP) principles.  
\- What is the purpose of the final, finally, and finalize keywords?  
\- What are the different types of memory areas allocated by JVM?  
\- What is the difference between String, StringBuilder, and StringBuffer?  
\- Explain the concept of method overloading and method overriding.  
\- What is the difference between abstract class and interface?  
\- What are the access modifiers in Java? Explain each with an example.  
\- What is the purpose of the super keyword?  
\- What are the key differences between an ArrayList and a LinkedList?  
\- Explain the concept of Java exceptions and the difference between checked and unchecked exceptions.  
\- What is a singleton class? How would you implement it?  
\- What are the differences between HashMap and Hashtable?  
\- What is the difference between ArrayList and Vector?  
\- What is the difference between synchronized method and synchronized block?  
\- What are the different thread states in Java?  
\- What is the volatile keyword in Java and when do you use it?  
\- What is the difference between wait() and sleep() methods in Java?  
\- What is a deadlock in Java, and how do you prevent it?  
\- Explain the difference between List, Set, and Map in Java Collections.  
\- What is the Iterator interface in Java? How does it differ from ListIterator?  
\- What is the difference between HashMap and TreeMap?  
\- How do you perform sorting in Java Collections?  
\- What are Lambda expressions and functional interfaces in Java 8?  
\- Explain the concept of Streams in Java 8 and provide an example.  
\- What is the difference between Optional and null?  
\- What is the purpose of default methods in interfaces?

These are the bare minimum that a Java developer must know.

The more experience you have, the more follow-ups to a question you'll have.

For example, if you were asked about types of memory areas in JVM, expect to be asked deeper questions on classloader, custom classloader, just-in-time compilation and other topics.

Save it as a reference for future\!

\=== POST 76 \===  
Rajya Vardhan Mishra  
View Rajya Vardhan Mishra’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Engineering Leader @ Google | Mentored 300+ Software Engineers | Building high-performance teams | Tech Speaker | Led $1B+ programs | Cornell University | Lifelong learner driven by optimism & growth mindset

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

In the last 15 years of my career, I’ve worked as a software engineer across Google, Paytm, Amazon, and multiple startups. 

Here are the most impactful lessons I’ve learned that will help you become a better developer and perform better in your career: 

1// Good Code Can’t Fix a Bad Product   
↳ You can write the cleanest, most optimized code  
↳ But if the product doesn’t solve a real problem, it won’t matter.   
↳ Understand the business impact behind what you build. 

2// Be the Most Helpful Person on the Team   
↳ The best engineers aren’t just technically strong, they enable others to do their best work.   
↳ Help teammates debug issues, improve designs, and write better code. It pays off. 

3// Look for Simple Solutions to Complex Problems   
↳ Complexity is easy. Simplicity is hard.   
↳ The best engineers know that less code \= fewer bugs. 

4// Success is Probability—Show Up More Often   
↳ A big part of success is just being present, trying, and iterating.   
↳ The more you put yourself out there, the luckier you get. 

 5// Ask Why? More Often—It Creates Clarity   
↳ Most technical problems are not about the best solution—they’re about the right problem to solve.   
↳ Keep asking why until you truly understand the root issue. 

6// There Is No Best Tech Stack—It’s All Trade-offs   
↳ Every tool, framework, and architecture decision comes with pros and cons.   
↳ The best engineers make informed decisions, not emotional ones. 

7// You Are Rewarded for Creating Value, Not Just Writing Code   
↳ You don’t get promoted for just writing more code.   
↳ You get promoted for solving real problems, improving systems, and making the team better. 

 8// Writing is How You Clarify Your Thinking   
↳ Engineers who write well communicate better, influence better, and get promoted faster.   
↳ Start by writing better PR descriptions, design docs, and technical explanations. 

9// Understand the Business, Not Just the Tech   
↳ The best engineers don’t just focus on code—they understand why the business needs it.   
↳ Learn how your company makes money and how your work impacts that. 

10// Find a Mentor or Coach—Don’t Struggle Alone   
↳ The fastest way to grow is to learn from someone ahead of you.   
↳ Don’t wait, reach out to senior engineers, ask questions, and get feedback. 

 11// Imposter Syndrome Never Goes Away—Keep Learning   
↳ Even the best engineers feel like they don’t know enough.   
↳ The difference? They keep learning, keep building, and don’t let fear stop them. 

Your career is built through small, consistent improvements over years.   
Apply these 11 principles, and you’ll move ahead…

\=== POST 77 \===  
Anshul Chhabra  
View Anshul Chhabra’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Senior Software Engineer @ Microsoft | Follow me for daily insights on Career growth, interview preparation & becoming a better software engineer.

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

This is the exact framework I used to answer system design questions in interviews at Microsoft, Google, Walmart & 5 other MAANG+ companies. 

I landed at Microsoft, and 6 years later, I still use these principles when designing systems at scale. 

1️⃣ Define the Problem First  
→ Don't jump into design, ask the right questions first.  
→ Define scale, trade-offs (latency vs. consistency vs. cost), and scope.

2️⃣ Explain It Like You Would to a Junior Engineer  
→ Break it down step-by-step before diving into databases or scaling.  
→ Keep it simple first, complexity comes later.

3️⃣ Deep Dive Where It Matters  
→ Focus on key areas: scaling, availability, real-time processing.  
→ Don't waste time on unnecessary details.

4️⃣ Identify Bottlenecks Early  
→ What breaks first? Single points of failure? Traffic spikes?  
→ Address issues before your interviewer asks.

5️⃣ Summarize Trade-offs Like a Pro  
→ Justify design choices, why this DB, why this architecture?  
→ Show that you think like an architect, not just a coder.

(I’ve written a detailed version of this framework, here’s the link, go read and apply it ↓)  
https://lnkd.in/dSHZZM6e

\=== POST 79 \===  
Anshul Chhabra  
View Anshul Chhabra’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Senior Software Engineer @ Microsoft | Follow me for daily insights on Career growth, interview preparation & becoming a better software engineer.

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

What I learned after solving 600+ LeetCode problems (300+ hours of efforts)  
(This will help you nail 80-90% of your coding interviews, if you do it right)

During my Bachelor days at BITS Pilani, I solved 600-700+ LeetCode problems to prepare for coding interviews. 

At first, it felt like a grind, just something I had to do to land a job. 

But looking back, those hundreds of hours weren’t just about interviews, they shaped the way I think, debug, and approach complex problems. 

Even now, when switching teams or tackling a new domain, the problem-solving muscle I built through LeetCode still pays off. 

If you're preparing for coding interviews, here’s what I wish someone told me before I started 👇 

1️⃣ The Order You Solve Problems In Matters More Than You Think 

A huge mistake? Jumping straight into graphs and dynamic programming before mastering the basics. 

🔹 First, nail the fundamentals: Arrays, Strings, HashMaps, Linked Lists, Trees, Binary Search.   
🔹 Then, go deeper: Recursion, Backtracking, Sliding Window, BFS/DFS.   
🔹 Finally, tackle the hardest ones: Dynamic Programming, Graphs, Tries, Bit Manipulation. 

Solving problems in a structured order will save you months of frustration. 

2️⃣ Quality Over Quantity, Memorizing Solutions Won’t Get You Hired 

I see people bragging about solving 1,000+ problems, but here’s the truth: 

150 well-understood problems \>\>\> 500 brute-force attempts 

🔹 The goal isn’t to memorize solutions but to recognize patterns.   
🔹 Every medium/hard problem is just a remix of a classic pattern.   
🔹 If you can’t explain why a solution works, you haven’t understood it. 

I’d rather fully understand 150 problems than struggle through 500 and still freeze in an interview. 

3️⃣ Patterns Are Everything, Learn Them, and You’ll Never Be Stuck 

LeetCode isn’t about learning 600 different solutions. It’s about seeing the same 20-30 ideas appear again and again. 

Here’s how I break it down: 

🔹 Medium problems → Identify the right pattern (Binary Search, Two Pointers, Sliding Window, BFS, DP).   
🔹 Hard problems → Usually have a single trick—once you spot it, 80% of the work is done. 

If you’ve seen House Robber 1, you’ll immediately understand House Robber 2 and Paint Fence. 

If you’ve solved Merge Intervals, you’ll recognize the same concept in Meeting Rooms, Insert Interval, and Skyline Problem. 

You don’t need to learn new ideas for every problem. You just need to recognize the remix. 

Continued Here (I’ve spent good time on this, read it once) : https://lnkd.in/gjCCdYe5  
…see more

🚀 I want to share my journey to switch from Optum (Service Based Company) to Amazon (Product Based Company) in just 6 months\! Here's how I made it happen:

📝 The Preparation Strategy

First, let me be transparent \- this wasn't an easy journey. It required dedication, consistent effort, and a structured approach. Here's my detailed roadmap:

Data Structures & Algorithms (DSA):  
\- Dedicated 2-3 hours daily to DSA practice  
\- Started with basic arrays and strings, progressively moved to complex topics  
\- Solved 300+ problems on LeetCode (focused on medium-level questions)  
\- Key topics: Dynamic Programming, Trees, Graphs, and Backtracking  
\- Pattern recognition was crucial \- grouped similar problems together

Operating Systems:  
\- Focused on core concepts: Process Management, Threading, Deadlocks  
\- Built practical understanding through small projects  
\- Key topics: Memory Management, File Systems, Process Scheduling  
\- Created notes summarizing important concepts  
\- Practice: Implemented a basic thread pool and studied Linux internals

Database Management Systems:  
\- Mastered SQL queries (joins, subqueries, window functions)  
\- Understood transaction management (ACID properties)  
\- Studied indexing and query optimization  
\- Practiced database design and normalization  
\- Built a small project implementing CRUD operations

Computer Networks:  
\- Studied TCP/IP stack thoroughly  
\- Understanding of HTTP/HTTPS protocols  
\- Network security fundamentals  
\- Socket programming basics  
\- Implemented a basic client-server application

Interview Preparation:  
\- Mock interviews with peers (3-4 per week)  
\- System design practice  
\- Behavioral question preparation  
\- Company-specific research  
\- Resume tailoring for each application

Tips That Worked For Me:  
1\. Consistency \> Intensity: Daily 4-5 hours of focused study  
2\. Quality \> Quantity: Understanding concepts deeply rather than rushing  
3\. Projects \> Theory: Built practical implementations of concepts  
4\. Community Learning: Joined Discord servers and study groups  
5\. Time Management: Created weekly goals and tracked progress

Resources That Helped:  
\- DSA: LeetCode, GeeksforGeeks  
\- OS: Operating System Concepts (Dinosaur Book)  
\- DBMS: Database System Concepts  
\- Networks: Computer Networking: A Top-Down Approach

Key Learnings:  
\- Don't just memorize, understand the concepts  
\- Practice mock interviews regularly  
\- Build a strong LinkedIn profile  
\- Network with people in target companies  
\- Stay motivated despite rejections

Remember, everyone's journey is different. This worked for me, but feel free to adapt it to your needs. Feel free to connect if you need any guidance or have questions\!

\=== POST 82 \===  
sukhad anand  
View sukhad anand’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Senior Software Engineer @Google | Techie007 | Opinions and views I post are my own

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

It might seem useless to be good at DSA just because it is asked in the interviews and never used in actual day-to-day work. But, that is not always the case. 

Robinhood uses graph algorithms like connected components to detect fraud. Here is how they do it:

1\. Robinhood’s graph-based fraud detection algorithms can be split into two categories: Vertex-centric Algorithms and Graph-centric Algorithms.

2\. Robinhood might start from one vertex that represents a known attacker and then identify risky neighbor nodes using BFS/DFS.

3\. If you have a certain node that is a known fraudster, then you can analyze all the nodes that share any attributes with the fraudster (IP address, device, home address, bank account details, etc.).

4\. With graph-centric algorithms, Robinhood analyzes the entire graph to search for relationships (so computations usually involve looking at the full graph).

5\. For example: Page Rank is an algorithm that measures the importance or influence of each node within a graph based on the number and quality of incoming connections. It assigns a score to each node, with higher scores indicating greater importance or centrality within the network.

6\. Robinhood can leverage Page Rank to identify the most influential or central accounts within a fraud network. By treating each node as a "page" and the relationships as "links," Page Rank can help Robinhood determine which accounts are likely to be the key players in a fraud scheme. Accounts with high Page Rank scores can be prioritized for investigation and intervention.  
…see more

\=== POST 86 \===  
Pratik Daga  
View Pratik Daga’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Principal Engineer | Ex Tech Lead-Asana & Staff Engineer-LinkedIn | Multi Family Real Estate

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

Canva wanted to add real-time mouse pointers.

This feature lets users see where others are pointing or working on a shared design. 

Here's how they did it:

At first, Canva used WebSockets and Redis. 

WebSockets sent data between users and our servers. Redis shared data between servers.

This worked well and handled lots of users. But it had downsides. All data went through our servers. It was a bit slow and costly to run.

So Canva switched to WebRTC. 

This technology lets users' computers talk directly to each other. When you join a design, your computer connects to other users' computers.

Your mouse movements go straight to other users, not through Canva’s servers. This makes things faster and uses fewer Canva resources.

Canva faced some tricky parts when setting this up.

For a while, some users used the old system and some the new one. 

Some older web browsers didn't fully support WebRTC. 

Sometimes connections between users can fail. 

Canva built systems to handle these problems and reconnect users.

Thus, by using WebRTC, Canva made their real-time mouse pointer feature faster and more efficient. This lets lots of people work together smoothly on the same design.

I write one post for software engineers everyday at 10\. Follow Pratik Daga so that you don't miss them.  
…see more

Practice these 42 LLD (Low-Level Design) problems, and you’re set for 90% of your interviews. 

If you practice them well, you can answer most questions that come your way.

Key tips for LLD interviews:  
\- Demonstrate your object-oriented programming skills.  
\- Highlight your ability to analyze and break down problems.  
\- Understand that no solution is ever fully complete.  
\- Be prepared to explain the reasoning behind your choices.

Your SWE level \== how vague your task is.

“Lower our bounce rate for EU pages.” \-\> Staff SWE

“P95 latency is too high across these 3 services.” \-\> Senior SWE

“Our DB is the issue, optimize these 4 SQL queries.” \-\> Mid-level SWE

“Speed up reads by adding an index to these 2 columns.” \-\> Junior SWE

Problems don’t always get harder as you grow.

Solutions become less clear.

\=== POST 89 \===  
Gaurish Baliga  
View Gaurish Baliga’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Avid Learner | Software Engineer @Rubrik | Ex-Google | Master @Codeforces | ICPC Asia West Finalist | ETH Global Finalist

1yr •   
1yr Visible to everyone

𝐀 𝐆𝐮𝐢𝐝𝐞 𝐭𝐨 𝐋𝐞𝐯𝐞𝐥𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐔𝐩 𝐘𝐨𝐮𝐫 𝐂𝐨𝐦𝐩𝐞𝐭𝐢𝐭𝐢𝐯𝐞 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐠𝐫𝐚𝐦𝐦𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐒𝐤𝐢𝐥𝐥𝐬

Are you looking to climb the ranks on Codeforces? Many programmers struggle with this very question. Here are some powerful tips that can help you achieve significant improvement:

𝐓𝐡𝐞 50 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐛𝐥𝐞𝐦𝐬 / 𝐑𝐚𝐭𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐏𝐫𝐚𝐜𝐭𝐢𝐜𝐞 𝐒𝐭𝐫𝐚𝐭𝐞𝐠𝐲

Stuck at rating X? Here's a simple yet effective strategy:

1\. Filter the Codeforces problemset to problems with a rating around X.  
2\. Solve 50-100 random problems within this range.  
3\. Once you're comfortable, move on to problems rated X \+ 100\.

𝐋𝐞𝐚𝐫𝐧𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐓𝐡𝐫𝐨𝐮𝐠𝐡 𝐑𝐞𝐚𝐝𝐢𝐧𝐠: 𝐁𝐮𝐢𝐥𝐝𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐛𝐥𝐞𝐦-𝐒𝐨𝐥𝐯𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐈𝐧𝐭𝐮𝐢𝐭𝐢𝐨𝐧

While video tutorials can be helpful, independent learning through written resources helps .

Here's how to get started:

1\. Make a habit of reading editorials after attempting problems.  
2\. Explore CP blogs and documentation for in-depth learning. Some great resources include:

CP Algorithms: https://cp-algorithms.com/  
USACO Guide: https://usaco.guide/  
Competitive Programming Guide: https://lnkd.in/d3jXPuw8   
Codeforces Catalog: https://lnkd.in/dNPUPV5J

𝐑𝐞𝐠𝐮𝐥𝐚𝐫 𝐂𝐨𝐧𝐭𝐞𝐬𝐭𝐬 𝐚𝐧𝐝 𝐔𝐩𝐬𝐨𝐥𝐯𝐢𝐧𝐠: 𝐒𝐡𝐚𝐫𝐩𝐞𝐧 𝐘𝐨𝐮𝐫 𝐒𝐤𝐢𝐥𝐥𝐬 𝐋𝐢𝐤𝐞 𝐚 𝐏𝐫𝐨

Competitive programming is a mental sport. Consistent participation is key to maintaining peak performance. Always:

1\. Regularly participate in contests.  
2\. After contests, upsolve problems you couldn't solve during the competition. Ask yourself and identify areas for improvement. Ask yourself: "What prevented me from solving this problem?". 

𝐁𝐞𝐥𝐢𝐞𝐯𝐞 𝐢𝐧 𝐘𝐨𝐮𝐫𝐬𝐞𝐥𝐟: 𝐏𝐨𝐬𝐢𝐭𝐢𝐯𝐢𝐭𝐲 𝐢𝐬 𝐊𝐞𝐲

Remember, if an average kid like me can make it, you can too\! Keep believing in yourself and never give up on your dreams. You will have negative deltas, but don't let them discourage you. Keep grinding\!

Keep Learning,  
Gaurish Baliga

\=== POST 90 \===  
Jim McMaster  
View Jim McMaster’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Java Software Engineer

2yr •   
2yr Visible to everyone

Several people have asked about books I like, so I will list a few. Just so you know, I have no relationship at all with the authors or publishers other than as a reader. Some books are Java-specific, but others are applicable to any language.

When I first started OO Design, I had no idea what I was doing. I read the Gang-of-Four patterns book twice without understanding it. Then I read Martin Fowler’s “Refactoring” (https://lnkd.in/gB-mBB7A) and the light came on. Most of the book is no longer useful, since it contains step-by-step instructions for performing refactorings. Now you just do them with a key-press in your IDE. But the first few chapters are pure gold\! They explain \*why\* you want to refactor and contain loads of OO knowledge. He lists code smells that indicate a refactoring is needed.

Most of my unit testing paper is based on ideas in Jeff Langr’s “Pragmatic Unit Testing” (https://lnkd.in/g3A7kaHE). It has lots of great ideas.

I am a proponent of Test Driven Development (TDD). I like David Astels’ “Test Driven Development: A Practical Guide” (https://lnkd.in/gAq4WRmX). It shows how to do TDD for a project start-to-finish.

You shouldn’t refactor without having unit tests in place as a safety net, right? But what if you have a code base that doesn’t have tests and it is impossible to write them? Michael Feathers talks about this in “Working Effectively With Legacy Code” (https://lnkd.in/gCSvihYP). In his terms, legacy code means any code without tests. He shows some relatively safe refactorings you can do in order to make testing possible.

I learned a lot about newer Java features like Streams and lambdas from “Modern Java in Action” by Alan Mycroft, Mario Fusco, and Raoul-Gabriel Urma (https://lnkd.in/gue4hzAF). 

Robert Martin wrote a really good book. “Clean Code” (https://lnkd.in/gByKmaQX). It has great tips on keeping your code clean.

Another good book in the same vein is “Code Simplicity” by Max Kanat-Alexander (https://lnkd.in/gFAC7xyV), which is available as a free download from the author’s site.

Josh Bloch wrote a lot of the Java libraries at Sun, and also a great book, “Effective Java” (https://lnkd.in/gN9Za5N3). He shows how to use Java in the best way.

Joshua Kerievsky wrote “Refactoring to Patterns” (https://lnkd.in/gssFCftK) as a follow-on to the Fowler book. Sometimes you have code that doesn’t use a pattern when it should. Other times you have over-engineered code which uses a pattern when it shouldn’t. Kerievsky shows how to move to, toward or away from a pattern as appropriate.

I hope you find these helpful. Feel free to add your own suggestions in the comments.

…see more

Refactoring

martinfowler.com

\=== POST 93 \===  
Anjali Viramgama  
View Anjali Viramgama’s profile  
   
• 2nd  
Software Engineer | Tech, AI & Career Creator (500k+) | Ranked 5th in the World’s Top Female Tech Creators on Instagram | Top 1% LinkedIn Creator | Featured on Forbes, Linkedin News & Adobe Live

2yr •   
2yr Visible to everyone

Here’s the exact plan I would follow if I was a new grad looking for a job in 2024: 

1\. Expand your network

Start by sending 20 LinkedIn requests per day (with notes) to people you look up to. You can request coffee chats, referrals, or interviews depending on the person. You are most likely to get a reply from a person who is active (you can check their Activity tab under their profile) and also doesn’t have thousands of followers (their inbox is usually clogged).

There are some great free Google Chrome extensions like “Pathrise Career Connect” that helps you find company contacts and generate messages with AI. 

2\. Take AI courses

With the pace at which AI is growing, the best software engineers will be the ones who can efficiently use AI to perform their tasks faster. Get familiar with coding tools like copilot and learn prompt engineering via online courses. Some of my favorites are the free courses taught by Microsoft Learn and Google Cloud Skills Boost (links in the comments).

3\. Find a mentor

If you enjoy a coffee chat with someone, you can request them to be your mentor. This could mean anything \- help with mock interviews, resume reviews, or weekly/monthly check-ins. 

An easier way to find a mentor would be using the online platform, Pathrise They have thousands of mentors (at top companies) for you to choose from, and you can sign up to have weekly meetings with advisors from industry-leading companies such as Google, Facebook, LinkedIn, Amazon, etc. You only pay AFTER you get hired, so you have nothing to lose. 

I have partnered with them to help more Job-Seekers, and you can apply in under 30 seconds here: https://lnkd.in/gv6xXS9T

4\. Polish DSA skills

Coding in an interview is very different from real-life software development. Go on Google and search “Blind 75 LeetCode Questions” to review the most frequently asked questions by tech companies. Also check out the “93 Software Engineer Interview Questions from Top Tech Companies” for practicing company-specific questions (links are in the comments)

5\. Mass apply

With hiring freezes and layoffs, now is NOT the time to be picky when it comes to applying. Apply everywhere, you can choose and reject companies AFTER you have multiple offers. 

What tips can you add to the list?

𝐄𝐦𝐛𝐚𝐫𝐤𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐨𝐧 𝐭𝐡𝐞 𝐋𝐨𝐰-𝐋𝐞𝐯𝐞𝐥 𝐃𝐞𝐬𝐢𝐠𝐧 (𝐋𝐋𝐃) 𝐉𝐨𝐮𝐫𝐧𝐞𝐲: 𝐑𝐞𝐬𝐨𝐮𝐫𝐜𝐞𝐬 𝐚𝐧𝐝 𝐈𝐧𝐬𝐢𝐠𝐡𝐭𝐬  
Whether you're a fresh graduate or haven't hit your 2-year mark yet, nailing down Low-Level Design (LLD) concepts is crucial. It helps you build systems that are efficient, scalable, and maintainable in the long run.

To help you on your journey, I'm sharing some resources that have been super useful for me in my own 2 years as a developer.

𝐋𝐚𝐲𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐭𝐡𝐞 𝐅𝐨𝐮𝐧𝐝𝐚𝐭𝐢𝐨𝐧:  
• 𝐌𝐚𝐬𝐭𝐞𝐫𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐭𝐡𝐞 𝐂𝐨𝐫𝐞: A firm grasp of Object-Oriented Programming (OOP) and SOLID principles forms the bedrock of successful LLD.  
◦ 𝐂𝐫𝐚𝐬𝐡 𝐂𝐨𝐮𝐫𝐬𝐞 𝐂𝐒: Object-Oriented Programming: https://lnkd.in/gwKtu-NX\]  
◦ 𝐒𝐎𝐋𝐈𝐃 𝐄𝐱𝐩𝐥𝐚𝐢𝐧𝐞𝐝 𝐰𝐢𝐭𝐡 𝐑𝐞𝐚𝐥-𝐖𝐨𝐫𝐥𝐝 𝐄𝐱𝐚𝐦𝐩𝐥𝐞𝐬: https://lnkd.in/gidGJ7SW\]

𝐋𝐞𝐯𝐞𝐫𝐚𝐠𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐃𝐞𝐬𝐢𝐠𝐧 𝐏𝐚𝐭𝐭𝐞𝐫𝐧𝐬:  
• 𝐄𝐦𝐛𝐫𝐚𝐜𝐞 𝐄𝐟𝐟𝐢𝐜𝐢𝐞𝐧𝐜𝐲: Employ established design patterns to effectively address recurring problems with optimal solutions.  
◦ 𝐆𝐚𝐧𝐠 𝐨𝐟 𝐅𝐨𝐮𝐫 𝐓𝐮𝐭𝐨𝐫𝐢𝐚𝐥: https://lnkd.in/gVGepW-G\]  
◦ 𝐄𝐱𝐩𝐥𝐚𝐢𝐧𝐞𝐝 𝐒𝐢𝐦𝐩𝐥𝐲: https://lnkd.in/gFzu3G5m\]

𝐓𝐡𝐞 𝐏𝐨𝐰𝐞𝐫 𝐨𝐟 𝐕𝐢𝐬𝐮𝐚𝐥𝐢𝐳𝐚𝐭𝐢𝐨𝐧:  
• 𝐂𝐥𝐚𝐫𝐢𝐟𝐲 𝐂𝐨𝐦𝐩𝐥𝐞𝐱𝐢𝐭𝐲: Utilize UML diagrams to visually represent a system's components and interactions, enhancing understanding.  
◦ 𝐂𝐥𝐚𝐬𝐬 𝐃𝐢𝐚𝐠𝐫𝐚𝐦𝐬: https://lnkd.in/g7GkRkQr\]  
◦ 𝐒𝐞𝐪𝐮𝐞𝐧𝐜𝐞 𝐃𝐢𝐚𝐠𝐫𝐚𝐦𝐬: https://lnkd.in/gXapbFc3\]  
◦ 𝐔𝐬𝐞 𝐂𝐚𝐬𝐞 𝐃𝐢𝐚𝐠𝐫𝐚𝐦𝐬: https://lnkd.in/gy2gYJCF\]

𝐃𝐞𝐥𝐯𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐃𝐞𝐞𝐩𝐞𝐫:  
• 𝐓𝐞𝐬𝐭-𝐃𝐫𝐢𝐯𝐞𝐧 𝐂𝐨𝐧𝐟𝐢𝐝𝐞𝐧𝐜𝐞: Ensure a design's robustness by effectively crafting test cases.  
• 𝐌𝐮𝐥𝐭𝐢𝐭𝐡𝐫𝐞𝐚𝐝𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐌𝐚𝐬𝐭𝐞𝐫𝐲: Understand how to handle concurrent processes for intricate systems.  
◦ 𝐂𝐨𝐧𝐜𝐮𝐫𝐫𝐞𝐧𝐭 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐠𝐫𝐚𝐦𝐦𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐢𝐧 𝐉𝐚𝐯𝐚: https://lnkd.in/gDFwdwrB\]

𝐏𝐫𝐚𝐜𝐭𝐢𝐜𝐞 𝐌𝐚𝐤𝐞𝐬 𝐏𝐞𝐫𝐟𝐞𝐜𝐭:

◦ 𝐇𝐨𝐭𝐞𝐥 𝐁𝐨𝐨𝐤𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐒𝐲𝐬𝐭𝐞𝐦: https://lnkd.in/gMcs6\_wY\]  
◦ 𝐏𝐚𝐫𝐤𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐋𝐨𝐭: https://lnkd.in/gKyQKgEH\]

𝐑𝐞𝐦𝐞𝐦𝐛𝐞𝐫: This is merely the beginning of the LLD journey. Continuous exploration, active practice, and sharing experiences and resources contribute to a supportive learning community. Let's collectively delve deeper into the world of LLD\!
